Title: John Wilson picks the best wines for Christmas Day from all the main retailers
URL: https://www.irishtimes.com/food/drink/2025/12/13/john-wilson-picks-the-best-wines-for-christmas-day-from-all-the-main-retailers/
Time Published: 2025-12-13T05:00:00Z
Full Content:
I am fortunate enough to celebrate Christmas twice each year, enjoying a Danish feast featuring pork and duck on Christmas Eve and the traditional Irish turkey dinner the following day. In the past, I did not open up my finest wines, but now the children have grown older so I am happy to serve some very nice wine. Usually, we enjoy a good mature Bordeaux on Christmas Eve, and a nice pinot noir or cool climate syrah on Christmas Day. I will cover sparkling wine next week, but a glass of something fizzy is a great way to get proceedings started and can be drunk throughout the meal. This is the time to open some of the fortified traditional Christmas wines. I also make room for a dessert wine of some sort. I will then alternate between an after-dinner glass of sherry with cheese and walnuts one evening, and a glass of Port the next, either with cheese or a generous slice of Christmas cake. My sherry will be a bottle of Lustau Tabanco Amontillado (â‚¬23.95, Mitchell & Son) and the Port a late bottled vintage, Niepoort LBV (â‚¬28-30, independents). Markets Hot Stocks Fear & Greed Index Latest Market News Hot Stocks President Donald Trump on Thursday ordered a review of two major proxy advisory firms â€” companies that advise shareholders on votes â€” that had previously drawn the ire of Tesla CEO Elon Musk and others in Corporate America. The executive order singles out the two biggest players in the market: Institutional Shareholder Services (ISS) and Glass Lewis. Proxy advisory firms like ISS and Glass Lewis provide recommendations on how major investors like pension funds should vote on corporate decisions and at shareholder meetings â€” sometimes to the consternation of other shareholders or even corporate executives. Trumpâ€™s executive order directs the Securities and Exchange Commission to review ISS and Glass Lewis specifically scrutinizing their use of diversity, equity and inclusion (DEI) and environmental, social and governance policies (ESG). â€œEven if no immediate rules change, boards, institutional investors and the firms themselves take notice,â€ Kerry Berchem, a partner at Akin and co-leader of the firmâ€™s corporate governance and activism practice, told CNN. â€œThis can influence behavior indirectly â€” firms may alter recommendations, increase transparency, or adjust methodologies to avoid conflict with regulators.â€ The order is a win for Musk, who has bemoaned ISS and Glass Lewisâ€™ role in advising shareholders. The advisory firms have previously recommended that investors vote against corporate decisions at Tesla like giving Musk a larger pay package. Some on Wall Street have also previously voiced concern about proxy advisory firmsâ€™ sway over shareholdersâ€™ decisions. JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon has argued against the companies, calling them â€œincompetent.â€ Elon Musk said he needs $1 trillion to stop â€˜corporate terroristsâ€™ from wresting control of Tesla ISS and Glass Lewis â€œuse their substantial power to advance and prioritize radical politically-motivated agendas,â€ including DEI and ESG, according to the order. These proxy advisory firms have offered research and advice that incorporates ESG investing, which has been slammed by many conservatives. Under pressure from the Trump administration, Corporate America has seen a broad shift away from DEI and ESG policies that were more widely embraced a few years ago. â€œPresident Trump and many Republican allies have made clear their concerns about â€˜woke capitalismâ€™ â€” the idea that corporations, investors, or advisors push political or social agendas (like ESG, DEI, climate policies) that he views as contrary to shareholder financial interests or traditional US economic priorities,â€ Berchem said. â€œPresident Trumpâ€™s move wonâ€™t immediately change proxy advice, but it signals scrutiny, assuredly rattles the industry and subtly shifts the power dynamic toward corporate boards,â€ she added. ISS, founded in 1985, and Glass Lewis, founded in 2003, advise major institutional investors like BlackRock, pension funds and a range of other asset managers. ISS and Glass Lewis are owned by companies in Germany and Canada, respectively. Institutional investors that manage up to trillions of dollars can invest in so many companies that, when it comes to annual shareholder meetings and votes, they need more insight on what to decide. Proxy advisory firms conduct research and advise on how to vote on decisions including, but not limited to, executive compensation, board structure, climate and environmental issues and potential mergers and acquisitions. â€œI question whether American corporate governance should be determined by for-profit international institutions that may have their own strong feelings about what constitutes good corporate governance,â€ Dimon wrote in his 2023 annual letter to shareholders. Glass Lewis has always operated with the highest ethical standards with our clients being at the center of everything we do.â€ Trumpâ€™s executive order highlights just how influential ISS and Glass Lewis have become, Lawrence Elbaum, an M&A partner at Sullivan & Cromwell and co-head of its shareholder activism defense practice, told CNN. â€œItâ€™s on the heels of efforts by different states and other constituencies to try to curb ISS and Glass Lewisâ€™ influence,â€ Elbaum said. â€œSo Iâ€™m not surprised to see the executive order.â€ Attorneys from Sullivan & Cromwell are representing Trump in a New York criminal case. Elbaum is not affiliated with the legal defense. The proxy advisory firms have come under pressure in recent months. Floridaâ€™s attorney general, James Uthmeier, is suing ISS and Glass Lewis, alleging the companies are in violation of state antitrust laws. Trumpâ€™s executive order also directs the Federal Trade Commission to investigate whether ISS and Glass Lewis are in violation of antitrust laws. Still, Elbaum cautioned, until any government reviews are completed, itâ€™s hard to know what exactly will happen to the advisory firms. â€œI would say this EO is an initial momentum toward a â€˜winâ€™ for Musk and Dimon, but ultimately we wonâ€™t know whether thereâ€™s been a â€˜winâ€™ until this plays out further as the government agencies do their work,â€ Elbaum said. Title: WATCH: James Fishback says foreigners taking American jobs is the beginning of the end

By WND Staff December 12, 2025 America is pushing its own workers out of the economy, and almost no one in power will say it out loud. James Fishback joins Elizabeth Farah days after launching his campaign for Florida governor to explain how visa programs, hedge funds, and AI expansion are dismantling jobs, homeownership, and family life. Fishback points to hard numbers and concrete policies. Only 15 percent of 30-year-old men now own a home. Corporations legally replace American graduates with H-1B visa labor. BlackRock and Blackstone outbid families for single-family homes and turn neighborhoods into rentals. Airbnb replaces neighbors with weekend tourists. AI data centers drive up electricity costs while threatening local water supplies. Elizabeth presses Fishback on what this actually looks like on the ground. Young Americans delay marriage because they cannot afford a home. Families lose bidding wars to hedge funds. College graduates train their foreign replacements. Communities hollow out as housing becomes a balance-sheet asset instead of a place to raise children. He lays out why states do not have to accept this arrangement, and why Florida could become the first test case for blocking labor replacement, taxing corporate home hoarding, and stopping AI projects that raise utility bills. This is a blunt, unsanitized conversation about who the American economy now serves, who it leaves behind, and why restoring jobs, housing, and sovereignty may start at the state level. Investing Sending You to Google News in 3 Â© NATO alliance flag on Europe map. North Atlantic Treaty Organization. High quality photo (Shutterstock.com) by Rokas Tenys Going into the final weeks of 2025, the S&P 500 has had another strong year. ETFs that track the S&P 500 have posted double-digit returns yet again for the year, with Vanguard S&P 500 ETF (NYSE: VOO) coming in year-to-date at 18.43% and SPDR S&P 500 ETF (NYSE: SPY) right behind at 18.34% at the time of this writing. Much of these gains can be credited to the multi-trillion net capital valuations that have swelled with Apple, Nvidia, Microsoft, and other Magnificent 7 AI stocks that festoon the S&P 500â€™s top 10. However, there are many household name companies that are not a part of the S&P 500 top 10, mostly due to their lack of AI development. A good number of them arenâ€™t even American. For example â€“ Nestle (chocolate), AstroZeneca (pharma), and LVMH (luxury goods) are all European companies whose products have garnered widespread use, and their respective stock prices have risen accordingly. Nevertheless, they are essential in the market, and all three can be found in the top 10 list of another ETF: the Vanguard FTSE Europe ETF (NYSE: VGK). More intriguing is the fact that VGK currently sports a year-to-date return of 32.97%, which is 79% higher than VOO and only a few points shy of doubling its returns. The Vanguard FTSE Europe ETF contains over 1,200 stocks hailing from the Eurozone and Scandinavia. Issued by Vanguard, the second largest asset manager in the world after BlackRock, the Vanguard FTSE Europe ETF tracks the FTSE Developed Europe All Cap Index, which covers stocks from the Eurozone and Scandinavia. Passively managed, its inception dates back to 3-4-2005. There are a number of reasons why VGK has been able to outperform VOO and SPY this year. It is admittedly an anomaly. Historically, it has usually lagged behind, sometimes by a substantial amount. However, 2025 contained certain events that helped to give VGK a big boost. A few of these include: Geopolitical Tariff Fears: In March, President Trumpâ€™s reciprocal tariff policy announcement caused a big drop in the S&P 500, since many believed the analysts and pundits that it would trigger massive inflation and supply chain issues. With WalMart and other US retailers relying on Chinese manufacturing for millions of their best-selling retail items, the concerns over a renewed trade war with China were understandably disconcerting. Europe, on the other hand, felt the impact much less severely. The initial trade conflicts largely were focused on China and India, so the comparative stability of European markets drew investors from all over the world who were seeking less volatility and unknown economic outcomes. Sector Preferences From Global Investors: The huge bull run on the AI focused Magnificent 7 stocks has created the first multi-trillion valuations of Apple, Nvidia, Microsoft, et. al. VGKâ€™s 44% weighting towards financials and industrials became appealing to many institutional investors this year who shared Burryâ€™s fears. Strength In Numbers: While the S&P 500 represents 500 of the strongest market-weighted companies representing a cross-section of US economic and financial health, the Magnificent 7 stocks are responsible for over half of the 18% gain YTD, making it a â€œlive by the sword, die by the sword scenarioâ€. By contrast, VGKâ€™s index, the FTSE Developed Europe All Cap Index, encompasses over 1,200 different stocks across multiple countries. Given the lower level of sector competition in each European and Scandinavian nation, each has its own territorial near-monopolies in various sectors, cumulatively making for multiple big winners all contributing to the overall index strength. As 2025 comes to a close, the S&P 500 is still not showing any signs of faltering, but Europe should be able to maintain at least some of its lead, provided it does not succumb to foolish policies or geopolitical in-fighting among EU members, such as Brusselsâ€™ attempts to compel Italy to accede to its migrant policy, which Prime Minister Giorgia Meloni is staunchly resisting. Title: Vanguardâ€™s $11T Crypto Reversal: Can 50 Million New Investors Push Bitcoin to $100K?

Vintage Tone / Shutterstock.com For years, Vanguard stood out as a major holdout even as other financial giants embraced digital assets. When the first U.S. spot Bitcoin ETFs launched in January 2024 after lengthy SEC battles, Vanguard actively blocked its customers from buying those funds. So what changed by late 2025? Vanguardâ€™s reversal came from shifts both inside and outside the companyâ€”new leadership played a key role. In July 2024, Vanguard appointed Salim Ramji as CEO, the first outsider to head the firm. Externally, regulations around crypto developed significantly. Under a more innovation-friendly administration, the SEC eased its stance and approved multiple spot crypto ETFs. Competitors like Fidelity, Charles Schwab, and traditional banks moved ahead with crypto offerings, creating competitive pressure Vanguard couldnâ€™t ignore. The firm still wonâ€™t launch its own crypto funds, sticking to its core indexing philosophy. However, it now serves as a gateway for clients to buy crypto ETFs from other issuers like BlackRock, Fidelity, and Bitwise. The sheer scale of Vanguardâ€™s $11 trillion clientele means the impact wonâ€™t arrive as a one-time tsunami, but as persistent demand. Fifty million new potential investors with trillions in assets donâ€™t create overnight pumpsâ€”they create steady, persistent demand that supports multi-year bull markets. Bitcoinâ€™s price jumped roughly 6% in a single day when the news hit, briefly breaching $93,000 before settling around $91,000. Within minutes of the U.S. market opening on December 2, a wave of buying pressure sparked a sharp rally, catching many traders off guard. The surge liquidated an estimated $400 million worth of short positionsâ€”bets that Bitcoinâ€™s price would fallâ€”in 24 hours, as pessimistic traders scrambled to cover. Thatâ€™s a sign some big players are positioning for Bitcoin approaching the $100K threshold soon. DUSAN ZIDAR / Shutterstock.com Many crypto analysts are bullish about what Vanguardâ€™s entry means for Bitcoin. They point out that Bitcoinâ€™s supply is fixed at 21 million coins, with over 19.6 million already mined. Any influx of new buyers can have an outsized effect on price given the scarce supply. With tens of millions of new investors now gaining easy access through a trusted platform, the pool of potential Bitcoin demand has expanded dramatically. Each major wave of institutional adoption in the past has coincided with a leap in Bitcoinâ€™s valuation. The late 2017 boom was fueled by retail and initial coin offerings. The 2020-2021 cycle saw Bitcoin soar to $69,000 amid retail frenzy and corporate treasury buys. The 2024-2025 cycle, after ETF approvals, sent Bitcoin to new highs around $126,000 in October 2025. Bulls argue that Vanguardâ€™s embrace could mark the next leg of Bitcoinâ€™s maturation, potentially driving it to six-figure prices on sustained institutional buying. Some optimistic projections suggest Bitcoin could climb to $150,000-$200,000 by the end of 2026 if mainstream adoption continues at this pace, meaning $100K might be just a stepping stone. Vanguardâ€™s entry has sparked a new wave of bullish Bitcoin predictions. Here are three possible predictions for the BTC price heading into 2026. In an optimistic scenario, Bitcoin enters 2026 with strong momentum as Vanguard clients allocate even modest percentages to crypto. If just 1% of Vanguardâ€™s $11 trillion flows into Bitcoin ETFs over the next year, thatâ€™s $110 billion in new capital. Combined with continued institutional demand and regulatory clarity, Bitcoin could push past $100,000 and test $120,000-$150,000 by late 2026. Outside Vanguardâ€™s influence, other catalysts support this case. U.S. regulators approved the first spot Bitcoin ETFs in January 2024, opening crypto to mainstream funds. ETFs have gathered roughly $121 billion in assets by December 2025. Assuming continued strong ETF inflows (several billion per quarter) and no major macro shocks, Bitcoin would likely follow a grinding uptrend toward new all-time highs. In a moderate scenario, Bitcoinâ€™s price rises steadily but without parabolic gains. Vanguardâ€™s customers remain conservative and allocate slowly. Initial allocation rates might be around 0.1% to 0.2% of eligible Vanguard assets flowing into crypto early on, translating to just a few billion dollars spread across various crypto ETFs over the first year. That alone wonâ€™t catapult Bitcoinâ€™s price to six figures overnight. The base case portrays Bitcoin making modest gains into 2026, aided by steady investment from Vanguard investors, institutional flows, and clearer U.S. regulation, but without the hyper-enthusiasm or panic of extreme cases. Bitcoin trades in a $90,000-$110,000 range for much of 2026, with occasional tests of $115,000-$120,000 during bullish periods. Cautious analysts urge a more tempered outlook if things donâ€™t go according to plan. Critics point out that Vanguard is late to the partyâ€”their clients missed out on huge gains from 2023 to 2025 while the firm dragged its feet, and those big early opportunities wonâ€™t simply repeat on command. Additionally, macroeconomic headwinds could dampen enthusiasm. If the Federal Reserve holds rates higher for longer or if recession fears intensify, risk assets like Bitcoin could suffer. The timing of Vanguardâ€™s reversal also matters. Bitcoin already rallied from $15,000 in late 2022 to over $126,000 in October 2025â€”much of the institutional adoption story might already be priced in. In a bearish scenario, Bitcoin could consolidate in the $70,000-$85,000 range through 2026, with brief spikes to $95,000 followed by pullbacks. The $100,000 milestone would remain elusive as profit-taking and cautious macro sentiment cap gains. Our top personal finance-related articles today. Your wallet will thank you later. Title: Geminid meteor shower set to light up Irish skies
URL: https://www.rte.ie/news/ireland/2025/1212/1548731-geminid-meteor-shower-ireland/
Time Published: 2025-12-12T16:18:35Z
Full Content:
A meteor shower is expected to light up Irish skies tonight, with around 20 times more shooting stars than normal. The geminid meteor shower is set to reach its peak activity tomorrow night and into Sunday morning. However, Blackrock Castle Observatory centre manager Alan Giltinan said tonight will be the best night to catch sight of a shooting star due to a poor weather outlook across the weekend. He said that there could be up to 10-15 shooting stars per hour, but up to 100-120 per hour is possible. From 10pm onwards tomorrow night, stargazers should look to the east to try catch sight of the dazzling display. We need your consent to load this rte-player contentWe use rte-player to manage extra content that can set cookies on your device and collect data about your activity. Please review their details and accept them to load the content.Manage Preferences Mr Giltinan told RTÃ‰'s News at One: "I would encourage people to go out and have a look, and you might get lucky." According to Astronomy Ireland, up to around 20 times more shooting stars could be seen. They say that rural areas will get the most spectacular views, but city dwellers should see plenty too due to the bright glow of the meteors. Astronomy Ireland explained that the geminid meteor shower is caused by tiny pieces of space debris that fell off an extinct comet or asteroid called Phaethon that goes around the sun every one-and-a-half years. Title: BeToken Transforms an 18-Year Business Into a Web3 Asset
URL: https://www.globenewswire.com/news-release/2025/12/12/3204733/0/en/BeToken-Transforms-an-18-Year-Business-Into-a-Web3-Asset.html

Title: ETFs May Be Blurring the Line Between Gambling and Investing

Time Published: 2025-12-12T13:00:00Z
Full Content:
Wanna bet? ETFs arenâ€™t for gambling, but a rise in risk-on products could prove irresistible to a population obsessed with sports betting. Emile Hallez emile@thedailyupside.com To paraphrase a variety of sayings about making money in risky endeavors, the easiest way to get to $1 million is to start with $2 million. Historically, gambling and investing have been handled differently by the average American. The latter, of course, is intended to build wealth over time. But as access to gambling has dramatically expanded in recent years in the US, with sports betting and prediction markets available on anyoneâ€™s smartphone, the behaviors and attitudes around it have arguably bled into the investing world. There is now a booming new menu of exchange-traded products that let investors take risky, short-term stances, and that development in the ETF industry has some observers sounding the alarm. â€œThere really are few parallels between investing and gambling, but there are many close parallels between speculation and gambling,â€ said Robert Johnson, professor of finance at Creighton Universityâ€™s Heider College of Business. â€œAnd, unfortunately, the line between speculation and gambling is not only getting blurred, but it is being eliminated.â€ There are examples in the investing world that resemble speculation or gambling, Johnson said. Those include crypto assets, picking stocks in the electric vehicles market and leveraged ETFs (which in some cases overlap with crypto and EV stocks). â€œThere is no way to value cryptocurrencies other than the greater fool theory â€” the hope that some greater fool will pay you more than you paid,â€ he said. â€œIt is the consummate bubble, and investors should stay far away from cryptocurrencies in general and bitcoin specifically.â€ A growing number of asset managers might disagree with that. Itâ€™s now common for traditional financial companies to offer access to them. And some firms even recommend small allocations to bitcoin for wealth management clients. The latest company to cave to the crypto craze, Vanguard, allows brokerage clients to buy bitcoin and other crypto ETFs, but it doesnâ€™t include memecoins. Itâ€™s also notable that Vanguard doesnâ€™t include leveraged or inverse ETFs on its brokerage platform. Broad market index ETFs are appropriate for most investors, Johnson said. Leveraged ETFs, though, which amplify gains and losses and often reset daily, are not, he noted. Those products are increasing in number and size: Robinhood and other brokerage systems have helped trading skyrocket in popularity, especially since the COVID-19 pandemic, when many people were trapped indoors and had an abundance of time on their hands. While stockpicking can be inherently risky, the stakes have been pushed higher with the availability of prediction markets, or yes/no derivative contracts for the outcome of almost any event imaginable. Itâ€™s also big business: Still, there are differences in the kinds of information people have and how they use it that can distinguish between gambling and investing, said Valeria Martinez, CEO of Bettingladies.com, an online community of women who bet on sports. â€œInvestors rely on fundamentals and disclosures, while bettors rely on performance data, models and market sentiment,â€ she said. â€œPerhaps where the two overlap is in the mindset. People today are simply more used to making probabilistic decisions in all areas of life, whether itâ€™s choosing a stock or picking a team.â€ A former CFTC enforcement attorney who now defends white-collar clients said risky behaviors that start while gambling can snowball into investing, and at great costs. â€œInvestors who slip into speculative, emotion-driven behavior become far more vulnerable to fraud, market manipulation schemes and high-pressure sales tactics,â€ said Braden Perry, partner at Kennyhertz Perry. â€œThe behavior that begins as entertainment trading can develop into misuse of margin, unauthorized trading or increasingly desperate attempts to recover losses. Those situations often lead to regulatory investigations or even criminal exposure.â€ What Could Go Wrong? Even for stocks with net positive returns, leveraged ETFs held for longer than a day can underperform those target stocks because of decay, caused by their daily resets, amplified losses during volatility and fees. But big losses can also happen quickly, as seen during 2020â€™s extreme volatility, when inverse VIX exchange-traded notes lost nearly all their value and closed, Creighton Universityâ€™s Johnson said. A more recent example is UK-domiciled GraniteShares 3X Short AMD ETP, which erased its value in a single day in October after Advanced Micro Devicesâ€™s stock jumped 38%. â€œIn the hands of amateur investors, leveraged ETFs and ETNs are weapons of mass wealth destruction,â€ Johnson said. Whatâ€™s most worrisome to Aaron Randak, founder of Golden Acre Wealth Management, is the great wealth transfer, in which younger generations stand to inherit tens of trillions of dollars from baby boomers. The success stories of a few people in prediction markets might be what younger people most often see, rather than the risks. And that could potentially impact investing behavior down the road. â€œIt could be an overall positive for the world to have these prediction markets, because it shines a light on the major events,â€ he said. When it comes to investing, leveraged ETFs can be very useful for highly experienced traders and institutional investors, but â€œthe younger investor doesnâ€™t have any clue what research is needed.â€ After a dive, the companyâ€™s stock is still up 8% in 2025, while both long and short leveraged ETFs are down. The worldâ€™s largest asset manager and issuer of the biggest crypto ETF is expanding into staked Ethereum. Is there room for anyone else? Title: U.S. SEC Gives Implicit Nod for Tokenized Stocks
URL: https://www.coindesk.com/policy/2025/12/12/u-s-sec-gives-implicit-nod-for-tokenized-stocks
Time Published: 2025-12-12T12:17:04Z
Full Content:
The U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) implicitly granted approval for some stocks and other securities to be tokenized and traded on blockchains. The Depository Trust & Clearing Corp. (DTCC), the world's largest securities settlement system, said on Thursday that a subsidiary, the Depository Trust Co. (DTC), received a no-action letter allowing it to offer a tokenization service on approved blockchains for three years. Tokenization is the process of representing stocks, bonds and other real-world assets (RWAs) as digital tokens that can be bought, sold and traded on blockchains, with the aim of achieving greater efficiency and faster settlement. Some of the biggest names in traditional finance (TradFi), including JPMorgan and BlackRock, have developed projects in this area, demonstrating the potential institutional investment that they can draw to blockchain finance. The Depository Trust Co.'s authorization applies to the constituents of the Russell 1000 index, exchange-traded funds (ETFs) tracking major indexes and U.S. Treasuries. It plans to begin rolling out the service in the first half of 2026. A no-action letter is a formal response from the agency to a company stating that the regulator will not take any enforcement action for a proposed activity. Title: Trajan Wealth: Balancing Growth with Safer Options
URL: https://www.wealthmanagement.com/investing-strategies/trajan-wealth-balancing-growth-with-safer-options
Time Published: 2025-12-11T20:35:39Z
Full Content:
Co-CIO David Busch discusses how the firm approaches risk/reward calculations. December 11, 2025 Trajan Wealth, a $2.5 billion RIA headquartered in Scottsdale, Ariz., has a broad mandate. The firm serves clients on almost every level of the wealth ladderâ€”from the mass affluent, which it defines as anyone with $500,000 to $1 million in assets, including in their 401(k)s, to high-net-worth and ultra-high-net-worth investors. In addition, the firm aims to be a one-stop shop for all of those clientsâ€™ needs, including not just wealth management, but retirement planning, estate planning, tax planning and insurance and annuity services, according to David Busch, Trajan Wealth co-Chief Investment Officer. Not surprisingly, that requires a careful balancing act, combining an acceptable level of riskâ€”for example, through allocations to private equity and private creditâ€”with some skepticism about popular products du jour. For example, the firm has stayed away from Bitcoin ETFs, considering crypto a highly speculative asset, though it may eventually consider Bitcoin buffer ETFs, Busch said. WealthManagement.com had a conversation with Busch, who worked as an institutional portfolio manager prior to joining the firm two years ago, about Trajan Wealthâ€™s investment strategy and how it juggles its various clientsâ€™ needs. This Q&A has been edited for style, length and clarity. Related:Fidelis Capital: Focusing on Returns Net of Fees, Taxes and Inflation WealthManagement.com: Can you give us a sense of your typical client? David Busch: Our clientele is segmented between three groups. One I would call the mass affluent. Think about the everyday investor that retires and has $500,000 to $1 million in their 401(k) or people that have that [amount] in investable assets. We also cater to high-net-worth and ultra-high-net-worth. And as a firm, we offer services above and beyond just wealth management. We have our wealth management team that includes investment management. We also have estate planning, tax planning and preparation, as well as insurance and annuities. The idea is to have everything under one roof, so that our clients can have access to all of those different services if they need them. WM: Can you break down your allocations for us? DB: We offer a wide variety of investment products. Everything from your typical asset allocation models that range from conservative to aggressive to managed equity models, where clients buy individual stocks based on models that one of my investment managers manages. They include everything from large cap growth to dividend growth and value. We have an alternatives sleeve, where I include our managed futures product, which is a product thatâ€™s designed to provide crisis alpha. Itâ€™s a trend-following product, and its ... within managed futures that follow 30 to 50 markets. Essentially, they attempt to make money whether the market is going up or down. The caveat is that there has to be a clear trend. Related:Citi Global Wealth at Work: Investment Guidance for Execs and Other Professionals On the private side, we have curated specific opportunities for our clients to invest as limited partners into specific private equity funds. The reason we do it that way versus using a fund-of-funds type strategy, is that we can work specifically with a private equity firm, and we can perform our due diligence. We look under the hood, we look at all the investments, and then our clients who are accredited or qualified can invest within those vehicles. In that space, we offer everything from a venture fund to real estate, infrastructure and private credit. This gives our clients opportunities to invest in some of these sectors based on their risk tolerance and time horizons. The last thing is that we have fixed-income strategies that range from ultra-short to intermediate. WM: How often do you update your allocations? DB: It really depends on the specific clientâ€™s risk tolerance and their specific needs. We review our client holdings on a quarterly basis, their financial situation, their upcoming cash flow needs, whether they have to pay for something like college education or fund a payment on a home, as well as their long-term investment goals. Related:The Hidden Flaw in Style Index Funds WM: What were some changes youâ€™ve made to allocations recently? DB: In this moment, what we are looking at is large cap growth, specifically large cap tech, which has outperformed value for the past 15 years. That was really driven by low interest rates, and now there are some concerns in terms of valuations for some of these high-tech companies. I still am a firm believer that tech is the way of the future, and we should be invested in large cap tech over the long term. Over the near term, we have to be cognizant of the valuations, but also the capital expenditures that are required to build out the AI infrastructure. I believe there could be some short-term volatility, specifically in the big names. Thatâ€™s why, right now, we are looking at providing more of a balanced approach for equity models. You have your large cap growth, but balance that with large cap value, specifically dividend payers, because that cash flow will help offset some of the volatility. And when we have market volatility, value will typically outperform growth when the market is going down. On the fixed income side, I am looking at the Fed and the cutting cycle. The front end of the curve is going to move down because the Fed controls the overnight interest rates. Theyâ€™ve also announced a cessation of quantitative tightening. They are now reinvesting proceeds back into U.S. Treasuries. What that will likely look like is that it will help dampen some rate volatility, but the front end is going to be driven by the changes in the Fedâ€™s overnight funds rate. The long end is going to be driven by supply dynamics. So, I think there could be some volatility, particularly on the long end of the curve. As a result, we are focused on the three -to five-year part of the curve. With everything thatâ€™s going on within the credit marketsâ€”the consumers are stretched very thin right now, we are seeing rising delinquencies and defaults on consumer loansâ€”how I translate that is: â€œInvest on the fixed-income side in the three- to five-year part of the curve, but go up quality.â€ That should include Treasuries, investment-grade corporates. For high yield, I would be very cautious and selective and ensure that the names we are buying have the ability to repay that debt. You have to do a complete credit deep dive, almost like an underwriting. WM: Do you work with outside asset managers on public equities and fixed-income investments? DB: We do it all internally. We have a pretty robust team on the investment management side. We have a bank of analysts, traders, as well as portfolio managers that are constantly researching the markets and positioning based on that research. So everything we do on the public side is in-house. We currently have six people on that team. WM: How do you choose asset managers on the private market side? DB: We perform due diligence on those asset managers. The reason we steer clear of fund-of-funds type strategies is because those structures are embedded with feesâ€”there is the private equity managerâ€™s fee, the mutual fund company fees, and then typically, an advisor fee on top of that. So, we are removing a layer or two of those fees. When we look at each one of these private equity firms, we are using the relationships that weâ€™ve developed over the course of our careers and doing the due diligence in terms of their background, their history, their wins, their losses and also the opportunity that is in front of us. We look at theseâ€”I would call them boutiqueâ€”private equity firms. We generally steer clear of the big private equity funds that are out there. We are intentionally specific in that middle-market space. The reason is that when you are competing against the BlackRocks, the Blackstones and the Apollos of the world, and they raise billions of dollars for a new fund, they have to put that money to work pretty quickly. As a result, there is a food fight for the biggest deals that are out there. When there is a lot more competition, it waters down the returns. For us, because we are looking in that upper middle market space, we are avoiding that competition from the big players, and it has historically provided our clients with the opportunity for better returns. WM: What types of returns do you look for in that space? DB: If itâ€™s a private equity fund, either a venture or a buyout fund, it has to beat the returns of the S&P 500, or at least we have to anticipate that itâ€™s going to beat the returns of the S&P 500. Because whatâ€™s the point of going into a private equity fund if it canâ€™t beat the index itself? If itâ€™s a real estate structure, we look at the income our clients can receive, as well as the project itself and the valuations at which we are buying. In some instances, our clients are able to get accelerated depreciation for their tax returns. On the private credit side, thatâ€™s one area I am very cautious. The biggest reason is that companies have turned to private credit to access the loans they need without going to a bank. Banks have a very defined box they can lend within, based on regulations. So the promise of a private credit fund is that they can work with a company to structure the debt in a way that makes sense for the company, and itâ€™s also speed-to-closing. What I am concerned with, is that with non-bank lenders, they are able to make loans that are covenant-lite. And what we have seen recently in headlines is that there have been some banks that participated in these private credit loans and took losses because the borrower had assigned the same collateral to multiple loans. When they failed, itâ€™s now impacting everybody who was lending against that one specific collateral. The nice thing about bank loans is that when a bank issues a loan, they have a lien on that collateral. And in private credit, thatâ€™s not happening, and with covenant-lite, there may be instances where the lender did not follow through with all the covenants that are required to keep their position safe in the event of a bankruptcy. The other issue with that space is that we just donâ€™t have optics into it. There is no way to really know the performance of the underlying loans. WM: Do you invest in cryptocurrency in any way? DB: We have not. Our stance has been that itâ€™s a speculative asset class. I recognize that there are many ETFs that are available, from BlackRock and Vanguard and State Street and multiple other companies. Weâ€™ve seen the volatility in Bitcoin over the past several weeks. Thatâ€™s not an asset class we necessarily want to have available to our clients. What I can see in our future is that there are some very interesting structures in the ETF space where the ETF is built to participate in the upside in the cryptocurrency growth, but while protecting the downside using derivative contracts. In that instance, there may be an opportunity for us to allocate a portion, but itâ€™s not something we go all in on. WM: Do you invest in international markets? DB: We do. We generally invest in developed markets. We steer clear of emerging markets. In international, weâ€™ll use ETFs. There are a few countries that we are overweight on. Essentially, India as a country has the demographics. Thatâ€™s going to be the next stop for the onshoring of American manufacturing that will shift from China. They are highly educated, they are a fast-growing country. Thatâ€™s more of a growth bet. But we balance that with some good old, standby, safe countries like Switzerland as an example. The currency there is strong, that market is very stalwart. WM: Do you hold cash? DB: Yes. All of our model portfolios have a portion thatâ€™s allocated to cash. Itâ€™s really for operational purposes. We try to keep that cash balance at a minimum, unless somebody has a very specific need. Title: JPMorgan Pushes Deeper Into Tokenization With Galaxy's Debt Issuance on Solana
URL: https://www.coindesk.com/business/2025/12/11/jpmorgan-pushes-deeper-into-tokenization-with-galaxy-s-debt-issuance-on-solana
Time Published: 2025-12-11T19:16:30Z
Full Content:
Global bank J.P. Morgan has arranged a landmark commercial paper issuance on the Solana blockchain, in a move that pushes real-world financial instruments deeper into public blockchain infrastructure. Commercial paper, typically issued through legacy systems, is a short-term debt tool that companies use to raise working capital. This one was structured onchain and settled using USDC, the stablecoin issued by Circle (CRCL). J.P. Morgan created the onchain token representing the debt and handled the settlement. Galaxyâ€™s investment banking arm structured the issuance. Coinbase acted both as investor and wallet provider, while Franklin Templeton, which has already created a tokenized money market fund, also invested in the token. The move underscores the rising institutional interest to use blockchain plumbing for traditional financial instruments, also known as tokenization of real-world assets (RWA) like debt, fund or equity. The process promises efficiency gains, faster settlement, proponents say. The tokenized asset market could mushroom to $18.9 trillion by 2033, BCG and Ripple projected. The trend has also gained support from U.S. regulators. SEC Chairman Paul Atkins has recently touted tokenization as a key innovation for capital markets, saying in a FOX Business interview last week that it has the potential to change the financial system over the next couple years. The issuance was the latest example of J.P. Morgan's push into blockchain and tokenized assets. The bank has been an early mover, developing JPM Coin in 2019 and launching its blockchain unit, Onyx, in 2020. That division, now integrated under Kinexys, conducted blockchain-based repo trades, cross-border payments and tokenized asset settlements with partners including BlackRock and Siemens. Title: Trumpâ€™s National Security Strategy Lays Out a Pragmatic China Strategy
URL: https://www.dailysignal.com/2025/12/11/trumps-national-security-strategy-lays-out-a-pragmatic-china-strategy/

You can find columns and interviews at HelenRaleighSpeaks.com or her Substack newsletter, Confucius Never Said. Follow Helen on Twitter: @HRaleighspeaks. The White House has released a new National Security Strategy whose central purpose is unambiguous: to ensure the United States remains the preeminent global power. At its core is a new and pragmatic China strategy. The document opens with a blunt indictment of the past three decades of American policy, especially on China. Successive administrationsâ€”Republican and Democratic alikeâ€”embraced the delusion that flooding China with capital, technology, and market access would liberalize the Chinese Communist Party and peacefully integrate it into an America-led rules-based world order. Instead, we financed and armed our most dangerous rival. The result is a near-peer competitor that now menaces American security, prosperity, and the free world itself. The new National Security Strategy discards that fantasy. It is grounded in a cold-eyed recognition that the United States must competeâ€”relentlessly and across various regionsâ€”while buying the time required to rebuild the industrial, technological, and military foundations that previous generations allowed to atrophy. In the Western Hemisphere, the administration is reasserting the Monroe Doctrine. Chinaâ€™s creeping penetration of Latin Americaâ€”$2.5 billion into Panama alone, second-place usage of the canal we built, Chinese leader Xi Jinpingâ€™s pledge to wire 33 regional nations into Beijingâ€™s orbitâ€”has been met with swift counteraction by the Trump administration. Earlier this year, the Trump administration successfully urged Panama to withdraw from a China-funded infrastructure initiative. Soon after, Hong Kongâ€™s CK Hutchison announced the sale of its controlling stake in two Panama ports to a group of investors led by American investment firm BlackRock. In April, Secretary of War Pete Hegseth reiterated the commitment to counter â€œmalign Chinese influenceâ€ in the Western Hemisphere. The new security strategy declares, without apology, that the United States will remain the dominant power in the Western Hemisphere and that non-hemispheric actorsâ€”China foremost among themâ€”will be denied military footholds and strategic resources in Americaâ€™s backyard. The document delves more deeply into Americaâ€™s China policy in its section on Asia. The Trump administration recognizes that the Indo-Pacific region accounts for nearly half the worldâ€™s gross domestic product by purchasing-power parity. Free and open Indo-Pacific is vital to our national interest. On the economic front, the administration seeks to a balanced trade relationship with China based on â€œreciprocity and fairnessâ€ to regain American economic independence. The goal is clear: â€œIf America remains on a growth pathâ€”and can sustain that while maintaining a genuinely mutually advantageous economic relationship with Beijingâ€”we should be headed from our present $30 trillion economy in 2025 to $40 trillion in the 2030s, putting our country in an enviable position to maintain our status as the worldâ€™s leading economy.â€ Some China hawks recoil at any language of â€œmutually advantageousâ€ relations, hearing in it the echo of past naÃ¯vetÃ©. They are not wrong to be skeptical. Beijingâ€™s definition of â€œwin-winâ€ has always meant China wins twice. Yet, total decoupling immediately is not feasible. The United States requires time to rebuild its manufacturing capabilities and secure its supply chains. However, a measured tone does not suggest that the administration will shy away from robust competition with China or neglect the essential interests of the United States and its allies. For instance, the National Security Strategy clearly commits to cooperating with allies and strengthening military capacity that can counter aggression in the First Island Chain near China, especially to protect Taiwan due to its strategic location in the South China Sea. What started as an experiment is now becoming a functional part of institutional infrastructure, led by banks and asset managers who arenâ€™t waiting for a tokenized future â€” theyâ€™re building it. One specific product category has emerged as the clear front-runner: tokenized money market funds (MMFs). Tokenized MMFs are quickly becoming the core on-chain liquidity instrument for institutions, treasurers, and sophisticated funds. They bridge traditional short-term U.S. Treasury exposure with digital settlement, programmable workflows, and real-time portability. Growth is real: And institutions are incorporating tokenized MMF into businesses: Momentum is building, but the real story is what comes next in 2026. 1. Regulatory validation and collateral eligibility The Commodity Futures Trading Commissionâ€™s (CFTC)Global Markets Advisory Committee recommended tokenized MMFs as eligible collateral, and Acting Chair Caroline Pham launched a dedicated initiative in late 2025 to advance tokenized collateral adoption. If tokenized MMFs become approved as eligible margin collateral, recognized for cleared derivatives, swaps, and repo and embedded into CCP and FCM rulebooks, then tokenized MMFs evolve from a cash-parking tool into core institutional collateral, the same category that fuels trillions in daily financing today. This is a major unlock for banks, brokers, hedge funds, and trading venues that need intraday settlement and programmable liquidity. 2. The â€œinstitutional legitimacyâ€ moment Seventy institutions, including State Street, Fnality, Franklin Templeton, and UBS, contributed to Global Digital Financeâ€™s November 2025 report and demonstrated that tokenized MMFs can be: 3. The rise of tokenized cash rails at major banks Until recently, tokenized MMFs could only be redeemed via traditional banking rails or stablecoins. That is changing quickly. In 2025, we saw: As tokenized cash rails mature, institutions will be able to move tokenized MMF to tokenized deposit and settlement cash within the same ecosystem, with no friction and no conversion back to legacy payment rails or stablecoins. That is the moment when tokenized MMFs stop being a crypto-adjacent product and become digital liquidity management blocks for institutions. 4. Regulatory momentum for U.S.D and EUR stablecoins While tokenized institutional cash rails are rapidly expanding, stablecoin policy and legislation are helping stablecoins become the default cash rail for public permissionless space: Once these frameworks settle and SMEs become more comfortable with leveraging stablecoins for cash purposes, tokenized money market funds naturally become the yield, collateral, treasury, and portfolio cash solution. The direction of travel is clear. Cash that used to sit in bank accounts or legacy MMF portals is now being re-packaged into programmable instruments that plug directly into digital asset rails, and tokenized money market funds are becoming the cash management and collateral solution for all tokenized cash forms: tokenized bank deposits, deposit tokens and stablecoins. 2025 was the breakout year for tokenized money market funds as an asset class.2026 looks to be the acceleration phase, when tokenized MMFs become a standard treasury, settlement, and collateral asset for institutions. - Harvey Li, founder, Tokenization Insight Q: Traditionally, U.S. ETFs follow Wall Streetâ€™s market hours and settle through their clearing houses. When markets never close, it changes the way you need to operate. The benefits of real-time markets means that those who can react first will be able to capture the bulk of moves in asset prices. Q: The tokenized asset market is still insignificant compared to the trillion-dollar U.S. ETF industry. How will BlackRockâ€™s participation contribute to the tokenization ecosystem? A: BlackRockâ€™s massive asset portfolio will instantly increase the overall value represented by the tokenized ecosystem. More than that, it brings credibility to all types of blockchain-based assets beyond just Bitcoin and Ethereum. Q: BlackRockâ€™s CEO, Larry Fink, has been bullish on asset tokenization and wants to tokenize almost every traditional asset. Texas v. BlackRock (E.D. Tex.) (BlackRock), a case in which 11 states claim that the institutional-investor defendants colluded to profit through coordinated output reductions at coal companies they partially owned, remains in its early stages, with discovery continuing through 2027. Already however, opponents of climate-risk mitigation have rushed to extract specious theories of antitrust harm from Judge Kernodleâ€™s partial denial of the motion to dismiss (the BlackRock MTD decision). Following procedural requirements, Judge Kernodle took as true plaintiffsâ€™ allegations that the BlackRock defendants joined climate alliances and pursued related mitigation goals that â€œnecessarily result in the reduction of coal output.â€ Judge Kernodle also found plausible plaintiffsâ€™ allegations of antitrust harm to consumers, as reflected in rising prices during a period of decreased production by the relevant coal companies. Judge Kernodle did note that BlackRock may possess a valid defense to these allegations, stemming from long-term declines in coal consumption. But interests aligned with the BlackRock plaintiffs already have assembled a house-of-cards antitrust argument that any institutional investor factoring climate risk into its business decisions should face heightened scrutiny on routine portfolio acquisitions. A neutral analysis of the BlackRock MTD decision does not justify imposing such legal burdens, which would hinder these investorsâ€™ capacity to compete against their climate-indifferent rivals. This post seeks to clarify: (1) why the BlackRock MTD decision remains far from proclaiming institutional investorsâ€™ every climate consideration a proven anticompetitive output reduction; and (2) why institutional investorsâ€™ â€œsolely for investmentâ€ exemption from antitrust scrutiny still permits these investors to promote a wide range of climate-risk mitigation measures. The BlackRock complaint contains multiple antitrust claims. The plaintiffs assert violations under Section 7 of the Clayton Act, claiming each defendant used its partial ownership of coal companies in a manner reasonably likely to reduce competition, to consumersâ€™ detriment. Plaintiffs also assert violations under Section 1 of the Sherman Act, claiming defendants collectively agreed to coerce the coal companiesâ€™ implementation of a coordinated output reduction (reducing coal supply in order to intensify demand, raise prices, and boost profit margins). And Plaintiffs assert violations under state antitrust laws (not covered by this blog post). Judge Kernodleâ€™s MTD dismissal notes that the Texas complaintâ€™s â€œfirst of its kindâ€ allegation of a â€œhorizontal agreement among investors to pressure competitors in another industry to reduce outputâ€ [italics in original] does not call for per se antitrust analysis (an approach which assumes the anticompetitive harms of a given activity, and only considers whether defendants engaged in that activity). Instead, the court will conduct an elaborate rule-of-reason balancing, to â€œdivin[e] the procompetitive or anticompetitive effectsâ€ of these defendantsâ€™ business arrangements. Judge Kernodle makes clear that â€œweighty economics questionsâ€ remain to be resolved in later stages of litigation. Those questions will likely focus on defining an appropriate market to measure the competitive effects of defendantsâ€™ actions, determining whether plaintiffs have persuasively pinpointed how defendantsâ€™ actions harmed competition in these markets, and assessing defendantsâ€™ claims of procompetitive benefits. To scrutinize antitrust defendantsâ€™ effects on competition, courts typically first define a particular market in which the purported harm occurred or might occur. Judge Kernodleâ€™s MTD dismissal notes that defendants did not contest â€œat this stageâ€ of litigation the plaintiffsâ€™ proposed focus on the markets for South Powder River Basin coal, and thermal coal. Yet defendants may have good reason to contest these market definitions at subsequent stages. Consideration of a broader market (one encompassing a wide range of energy sources that satisfy the relevant consumersâ€™ needs) could allow defendants to argue that modernization and diversification of product options in order to meet shifting economic conditions (such as mounting climate risk), have not, on balance, harmed energy consumers. By extension, defendants might call into question the status of coal-company output decisions as a but-for cause of consumer harm, by pointing to across-the-board price increases for a diverse array of energy products. Alternately, if defendants could show that rising coal prices coincided with increased demand for substitutable energy products (such as renewable power, nuclear power, and/or natural gas), this also may weaken the plaintiffsâ€™ claims of harm to consumers or to competition. The BlackRock plaintiffs will face additional challenges meeting their burden of persuasion as this case progresses. The defendants only need to poke holes in plaintiffsâ€™ theory of harm. The defendants might start, for example, from plaintiffsâ€™ argumentative reliance on: â€œdifferent responsesâ€¦exhibited byâ€¦publicly held companiesâ€¦whose shares Defendants acquired and who cut their output during a period of rising pricesâ€¦andâ€¦privately held companies, who raised their output.â€ This facile comparison between publicly and privately owned coal companies looks easily contestable. The plaintiffs must convince the court that broader economic trends would not have prompted these same disparities between public and private coal companiesâ€™ output. But given the escalating societal concerns and conspicuous financial risks associated with an emissions-intensive energy source like coal, public companiesâ€™ greater susceptibility to reputational costs (alongside their added disclosure obligations) may incentivize such categorical differences in output. That certainly offers a simpler account than the elaborate conspiracy plaintiffs allege, in which institutional investors promised transformational clean-tech innovations, pushed for robust emissions reporting while faking their focus on climate mitigation, and cynically captured inflated profits by peddling dirty fuels. The BlackRock plaintiffs also advance tenuous antitrust causation claims through their frequent conflations of defendantsâ€™ alleged coal-reduction, emissions-reduction, and net-zero pledges. This misapplies antitrustâ€™s output-reduction concerns to restraints on firmsâ€™ negative externalities (greenhouse-gas byproducts), rather than on their supplying of economic goods (carbon-based energy sources). To date, Judge Kernodle has taken as true plaintiffsâ€™ assertion that a pledge to reduce coal emissions â€œnecessarily means cutting coal production because â€˜there is no realistic path for a coal company to cut coal emissions other than by cutting production.â€™â€ Yet regardless of whether one doubts the fossil-fuel industryâ€™s pledges of near-term clean-coal deployment, we consider it unlikely that a merits-stage court analysis would give the plaintiffsâ€™ brusque, future-oriented pronouncement of â€œno realistic pathâ€ much factual weight in proving present market harms. We likewise find it ironic that Texas Attorney General Ken Paxton has signed off (both as a licensed attorney, and as an accountable public official) not only on this sweeping denial of clean-coal boosterism, but also on a recent letter celebrating President Trumpâ€™s â€œBEAUTIFUL, CLEAN COALâ€ [capitalization in original] agenda. Nor do AG Paxtonâ€™s questionable claims stop there. In fact, the BlackRock complaint comes closest to demonstrating an agreement to reduce coal output (rather than to reduce greenhouse-gas emissions) by repeatedly misrepresenting plaintiffsâ€™ own evidence on this topic. For but one example, the complaint brazenly cherry-picks from a Net Zero Asset Managers (NZAM) document, so that NZAM appears to call on its members (including each BlackRock defendant) to â€œimmediately ceas[e] all financial or other support to coal companiesâ€¦building new coal infrastructure.â€ Yet plaintiffs fail to disclose that the cited NZAM document does not require this particular approach. The approach is one of five climate-mitigation strategies that NZAM signatories are expected to choose from (other options include a â€œphase outâ€ of thermal-coal investments on an unspecified timeline, or â€œrestricting financing for unabated coal power generation, i.e. without carbon capture and storageâ€). Judge Kernodleâ€™s MTD decision, in turn, cites this NZAM quotation in multiple passages, at times reading it as an express NZAM requirement, rather than as a nonbinding option. Later stages of litigation will allow defendants to question whether membership in a climate alliance that permits an open-ended â€œphase outâ€ of coal investments has evidentiary value in a lawsuit claiming real-world market effects between 2019 and 2022â€”and whether plaintiffs established that evidence in bad faith. Former Congressmember (and ranking Republican on the House of Representativesâ€™ Antitrust Subcommittee) Ken Buck has catalogued further ways in which â€œthe facts dispute the allegations,â€ including: no relevant coal-company manager being removed by proxy vote during the complaintâ€™s timeframe; certain defendants never voting against a single director; and purportedly colluding defendants consistently voting differently from each other. Should plaintiffs navigate these impediments to presenting a persuasive causal chain that culminates in anticompetitive effects, they still will need to address defendantsâ€™ procompetitive defenses. Multiple procompetitive rationales for corporate climate pledges present themselves, particularly for pledges to reduce emissions. To begin with, given regulatory uncertainty in the U.S. on coal emissions, as well as increasingly strict greenhouse-gas regimes in other advanced economies, reducing industry emissions (especially through lower emissions intensity) may facilitate continued coal production, under whatever constraints the industry finds itself facing. Moreover, the net-zero commitments in this caseâ€™s climate pledges need not even commit a signatory to reduced coal emissions (let alone reduced coal output), since by definition â€œnet-zeroâ€ calculations balance GHG additions to, and removals from, the atmosphere (with possibilities for offsets). Defendants also may argue that collective emissions pledges improve the quality of each investor firmâ€™s offerings, and follow antitrust law on standard-setting by â€œencourag[ing] competition with cleaner, more innovative, or more transparent products.â€ Along related lines, the Department of Justiceâ€™s Antitrust Division and the Federal Trade Commissionâ€™s (the Antitrust Agencies) statement-of-interest filing on the BlackRock MTD sets the stage for a green-transition procompetitive defense, particularly in investment markets: â€œan institutional investorâ€¦could advocate in favor of the business in which it ownsâ€¦stock to exit one market in favor of another, more profitable market [and] could even pressure the management of the firm to undertake such a transition [so long as this transition] would reduce output in the first market in service of achieving higher profits in another.â€ This logic leaves ample room for institutional-investor defendants with substantial coal holdings to persuade the court that they did not seek to self-destruct by advocating a net-zero transitionâ€”but instead to increase profits (and improve their own investment products) by calling for energy-sector innovation that better fits evolving economic circumstances. Amid these daunting challenges still faced by the BlackRock plaintiffs, climate denialists already have doubled down on dubious antitrust rationales for stifling emissions-mitigation and disclosure initiatives. The â€œwoke alertâ€ advocacy organization Consumersâ€™ Research, for example, sent a September letter to the Antitrust Agencies, celebrating Judge Kernodleâ€™s â€œvindicat[ion]â€ of the agenciesâ€™ BlackRock statement, which primarily argued that defendants overstated antitrust lawâ€™s exemption from Section 7 scrutiny, provided to stock acquisitions made â€œsolely for investment.â€ Consumersâ€™ Research highlights the Antitrust Agenciesâ€™ assertion that â€œan investment is not â€˜solely for investmentâ€™ if an investor has an intent to use stock to influence significantly or control management of the target firm.â€ Yet from this measured premise, Consumersâ€™ Research mischaracterizes many reasonable business efforts to reduce climate risk as suspicious â€œmixed motiveâ€ practices demanding enhanced antitrust scrutiny. These suspect practices would include: an institutional investorâ€™s public statement encouraging clarity in corporationsâ€™ greenhouse-gas reduction targets; an institutional investorâ€™s voting of shares against incumbent directors who fail to pursue net-zero alignment; and even an investorâ€™s mere vote for annual emissions disclosures like those increasingly required in most advanced economies. This legal pivot from anticompetitive harms and managerial control (in the Antitrust Agenciesâ€™ statement), to a much broader array of climate-minded â€œbusiness decisions,â€ would vastly restrict the shareholder rights of institutional investors and their clients, while harming competition in investment markets by capriciously targeting firms that engage in broad categories of risk analysis. By contrast, the Antitrust Agenciesâ€™ BlackRock statement offered multiple indications that â€œconferring with directors and management on best practices forâ€¦oversight processes,â€ or using â€œinvestment holdings and market status to influence or changeâ€¦public reporting practices,â€ does not inevitably depart from Section 7 solely-for-investment standards. Rather, these actions confirm the â€œcritical role in corporate governance mattersâ€ played by institutional investors. Subsequent rounds of the Texas v. BlackRock litigation hopefully will clarify that such investors need not sit on their hands as the world burns. Cynthia Hanawalt is the Director of Climate and Business Law at the Sabin Center for Climate Change Law. Andy Fitch is a Climate and Business Law Fellow at the Sabin Center for Climate Change Law at Columbia Law School. Title: BlackRockâ€™s move into Ethereum staking signals a brutal new fee regime that mid-tier operators wonâ€™t survive
URL: https://cryptoslate.com/blackrocks-move-into-ethereum-staking-signals-a-brutal-new-fee-regime-that-mid-tier-operators-wont-survive/
Time Published: 2025-12-11T14:00:39Z
Full Content:
BlackRockâ€™s filing raises questions on slashing risk, custody waterfalls and yield passthrough. We chart three scenarios for fees and liquidity. Cover art/illustration via CryptoSlate. Image includes combined content which may include AI-generated content. BlackRock filed for a staking-enabled Ethereum (ETH) trust on Dec. 5, which reframes the question of what kind of risk stack institutional investors will accept. The document outlines a structure that requires allocators to price three distinct failure modes simultaneously. First, protocol-level slashing penalties can hit the trust's vault account with no guarantee of full recovery. Second, a multi-entity custody arrangement where a trade credit lender holds first-priority liens over trust assets and can liquidate positions if credits aren't repaid on time. Third, a variable yield stream in which the sponsor controls how much ether is staked versus held in liquid form, creating a direct tension between the trust's redemption needs and the sponsor's staking-related fees. The filing seems like a bet that institutional buyers will treat Ethereum validator risk the way they've learned to treat counterparty risk in prime brokerage: as manageable, diversifiable, and worth paying someone else to monitor. BlackRock plans to stake 70% to 90% of the trust's ETH through â€œprovider-facilitated staking,â€ selecting operators based on uptime and slashing history. The S-1 acknowledges that slashed assets are debited directly from the vault and that any compensatory payments from providers may not fully cover losses. The language leaves open how much residual risk investors ultimately absorb and whether the sponsor would cut staking levels materially if validator risk climbs. That matters because slashing doesn't hurt through the raw ETH destroyed, but through the second-order behavior it triggers. An isolated slashing event is written off as an operator-quality problem, while a correlated slashing event, such as a client bug that takes down validators across multiple providers, becomes a system-trust problem. Exit queues lengthen because Ethereum's validator churn is rate-limited. Liquid staking tokens can trade at steep discounts as holders scramble for immediate liquidity while market makers pull back. Institutional allocators are demanding clearer indemnities, proof of multi-client failover, and explicit backstops, which are pushing fees higher and separating â€œinstitutional-gradeâ€ operators from everyone else. The custody structure adds another layer. The trust routes assets through an ETH custodian, a prime execution agent, and a trade credit lender, with the option to move to an additional custodian if needed. To secure trade credits, the trust grants a first-priority lien over both its trading and vault balances. If a credit isn't repaid on time, the lender can seize and liquidate assets, burning through the trading balance first. The dynamic creates a claim-priority question in fast markets: who gets paid when, and what happens if service relationships are restricted or terminated? The filing notes that insurance programs may be shared across clients rather than dedicated to the trust, which weakens the comfort level for large allocators. Settlement timing adds friction. Moving ETH from the vault to the trading balance occurs on-chain to prevent network congestion from delaying redemptions. That's not theoretical, as Ethereum has seen periodic gas spikes that would bottleneck large fund flows. On yield, the trust will distribute staking consideration net of fees at least quarterly, but the exact fee split remains redacted in the draft filing. The S-1 flags a conflict of interest: the sponsor earns more when staking levels run higher, but the trust needs liquidity to meet redemptions. There's no guarantee of rewards, and past returns don't predict future ones. The filing implicitly prices three scenarios, each with different effects on validator fees and liquidity. Under normal operations, staking looks boring. Exit queues stay manageable, withdrawals happen on schedule, and liquid staking tokens trade near fair value with small discounts that reflect general risk appetite. Additionally, operator fees stay tight as providers compete on uptime, client diversity, and reporting quality rather than charging explicit insurance premiums. Reputation and operational diligence drive pricing more than tail risk. A minor, isolated slashing event nudges the equilibrium but doesn't break it, causing only a small direct economic loss. Some providers quietly rebate fees or absorb the hit to preserve institutional relationships, and demand drifts toward higher-assurance operators. The result is a modest fee dispersion between top-tier and mid-tier setups. Liquid staking token discounts might widen briefly, but liquidity mechanics stay smooth. The effect typically fades within days or weeks unless it exposes deeper operational flaws. A major, correlated slashing event resets risk pricing entirely, and institutional allocators demand stronger multi-client diversification, proof of failover, and explicit slashing backstops. The best-capitalized or most trusted operators gain pricing power and can charge higher fees. Exit queues lengthen because Ethereum limits the number of validators who can leave per epoch. Liquid staking tokens trade at deep discounts as holders chase immediate liquidity and market makers protect themselves against uncertain redemption timing and further losses. The system can appear liquid on paper while feeling illiquid in practice. Confidence and pricing can take weeks to months to normalize, even after the technical issue resolves. A staked Ethereum ETF will likely operate in the â€œnormal-opsâ€ regime most of the time, but the market will embed a small haircut into its staking yield to account for tail risk. That haircut widens in a major slashing scenario due to both lower expected net yields and a higher liquidity premium demanded by investors. The question isn't whether BlackRock can execute the mechanics, but whether the structure shifts enough demand toward â€œinstitutional-gradeâ€ staking to create a new fee tier and liquidity regime. If it does, the validators who win institutional flows will be the ones who can credibly price and manage correlated risk, not just run nodes reliably. The losers will be mid-tier operators who can't afford the insurance, reporting infrastructure, or client diversification that allocators will start requiring. Wall Street will pay for Ethereum yield if someone else owns the operational and protocol risk. Validators now have to decide whether they want to compete for that business or let the world's largest asset manager pick their replacements. PS: I am no fan of Vladimir Putin, but the line chart of GDP growth explains much. Not to be overlooked. Is Covington & Burling a premier firm in international litigation? And does their listing as a Band Four national firm on the Chambers list support that they are not? I donâ€™t know who at Covington wrote the memo referenced above and I havenâ€™t read the memo. That being said I worked at Covington as a litigator and at one of the higher ranked firms listed on the Chambers ranking for international litigation. Iâ€™ve also advised law firms about how to be recognized by Chambers, both as law firms and as individual attorneys (which Chambers also ranks). My overwhelming experience is that any firm mentioned by Chambers and many law firms not recognized by Chambers do very careful work, especially when litigation departments send memos about the law to clients. Covington has been around since 1919. If their lawyers write a memo that they think the risk of litigation is low because of lack of jurisdiction, there is a very high probability that there is at least a colorable argument supporting that conclusion. That doesnâ€™t mean the conclusion in the memo will prove correct, but it is unlikely to be just made up to tell a client what it wants to hear. And there is no difference between a Band One firm and a Band Four firm in Chambers in this regard. Chambers has its quirks, but itâ€™s people interview law firm clients as the basis of creating their rankings. If you are a band four firm nationally in a given practice area, that means you are in the top 10 to 15 firms in the whole country. That is a signal to in-house lawyers at large corporate law departments that you are very good in that practice area. Again I donâ€™t know specifics about this particular memo, but itâ€™s a stretch to conclude that a law firm just manufactured a legal opinion desired by a client because of its Chambers ranking. Band 4 out of 5 is low, not high, relative to the other big firms. It means they are in the running as a reasonable choice and might be good on a horses-for-course basis All the firms in the higher bands are above them. And the ranking was for what amounted to their international practice compared to other big US firms, not international litigation and not international financial service. As indicated there are very strong UK firms in international legal works, such as Freshfields in arbitration And I have been a Covington client, as in I retained them when I was head of M&A at Sumitomo. They are not all that strong in litigation. There are specialist firms (and not small ones, BTW) that are the killers in that field. Title: Ukraine Talks Cover Peace Plan, Security Guarantees, Economic Recovery - Reports
URL: https://www.globalsecurity.org/wmd/library/news/ukraine/2025/12/ukraine-251210-sputnik04.htm
Time Published: 2025-12-11T10:18:50Z
Full Content:
Sputnik News 20251210 MOSCOW (Sputnik) - Negotiations on the Ukraine settlement revolve around discussions on three documents, namely a US-proposed peace plan, a security guarantees agreement, and an economic recovery plan for the post-conflict period, The Washington Post newspaper reported, citing US and Ukrainian officials. The talks remain far from concluded, the report said on Tuesday. Ukraine and its European allies plan to present further revisions to the plan on Wednesday, it added. Additionally, US officials are in discussions with Larry Fink, CEO of financial giant BlackRock, about reviving the company's proposal for a Ukrainian reconstruction fund that could raise $400 billion, The Washington Post reported. The World Bank may also be involved, the report said. Since mid-November, the US has been promoting a new peace proposal for Ukraine. On December 2, Russian President Vladimir Putin received US special envoy Steve Witkoff and US President Donald Trump's son-in-law, Jared Kushner, in the Kremlin. The US representatives' visit to Russia was related to the discussion of the US peace plan for Ukraine. US President Donald Trump said later that he was disappointed that Volodymyr Zelenskyy had not read the peace proposal for the Ukraine conflict. On Tuesday, the Financial Times newspaper reported that Witkoff and Kushner gave Zelenskyy "days" to come up with a response to the peace plan. Zelenskyy reportedly told his European allies that Witkoff and Kushner pressured him during a two-hour phone call on Saturday to respond to the plan that demands Kiev accept territorial losses in exchange for US security guarantees, with Trump aiming to finalize the deal "by Christmas."
